	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Sugar</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><span aria-disabled='false' title='Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page.' class='flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget'></span><span id='mw-fr-revisiontoggle' aria-disabled='false' title='show/hide details' class='fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget'></span></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Sugar">reviewed</a> on <i>25 September 2019</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the class of sweet-flavored substances used as food. For common table sugar, see <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Sugar_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Sugar (disambiguation)">sugar (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Lump sugar" redirects here. For the South Korean film, see <a href="/wiki/Lump_Sugar" title="Lump Sugar">Lump Sugar</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Generic name for sweet-tasting, soluble carbohydrates</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/220px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/330px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg/440px-Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1149" data-file-height="1068" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucre_blanc_cassonade_complet_rapadura.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sugars (clockwise from top-left): <a href="/wiki/White_sugar" title="White sugar">white refined</a>, unrefined, unprocessed cane, <a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">brown</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Sugar</b> is the generic name for <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweet-tasting</a>, soluble <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, many of which are used in <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>. The various types of sugar are derived from different sources. Simple sugars are called <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> and include <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (also known as dextrose), <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">galactose</a>. "Table sugar" or "granulated sugar" refers to <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharide</a> of glucose and fructose. In the body, sucrose is <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysed" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrolysed">hydrolysed</a> into fructose and glucose.
</p><p>Sugars are found in the tissues of most plants, but sucrose is especially concentrated in <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>, making them ideal for efficient commercial <a href="/wiki/Extract" title="Extract">extraction</a> to make refined sugar. Sugarcane originated in tropical Indian subcontinent and Southeast Asia,<sup id="cite_ref-Moxham2002_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moxham2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and is known of <a href="/wiki/History_of_sugar#The_spread_of_sugarcane_cultivation" title="History of sugar">from before 6,000 BP</a>, sugar beet was <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet#Creation" title="Sugar beet">first described in writing</a> (1575) by <a href="/wiki/Olivier_de_Serres" title="Olivier de Serres">Olivier de Serres</a> and originated in <a href="/wiki/Beta_vulgaris#Distribution_and_habitat" title="Beta vulgaris">southwestern, northern and Southeast Europe along the Atlantic coasts and the Mediterranean Sea, in North Africa, Macaronesia, to Western Asia</a>.  In 2016, the combined world production of those two crops was about two billion <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>. Other disaccharides include <a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">maltose</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malted</a> grain) and <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> (from milk). Longer chains of sugar molecules are called <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>. Some other chemical substances, such as <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sugar_alcohol" title="Sugar alcohol">sugar alcohols</a>, may have a sweet taste, but are not classified as sugar.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg/220px-Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="438" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg/330px-Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg/440px-Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1590" data-file-height="3169" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zuckerplastik_zur_Freiheitsidee_1880_Zucker-Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German sugar sculpture, 1880</div></div></div>
<p>Sucrose is used in prepared foods (e.g. cookies and cakes), is sometimes <a href="/wiki/Added_sugar" title="Added sugar">added to commercially available beverages</a>, and may be used by people as a sweetener for foods (e.g. toast and cereal) and beverages (e.g. coffee and tea). The average person consumes about 24 kilograms (53&#160;lb) of sugar each year, or 33.1 kilograms (73&#160;lb) in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a>, equivalent to over 260 <a href="/wiki/Food_calorie" class="mw-redirect" title="Food calorie">food calories</a> per day. As sugar consumption grew in the latter part of the 20th&#160;century, researchers began to examine whether a diet high in sugar, especially refined sugar, was damaging to <a href="/wiki/Human_health" class="mw-redirect" title="Human health">human health</a>. Excessive consumption of sugar has been implicated in the onset of <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus" class="mw-redirect" title="Diabetes mellitus">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">dementia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tooth_decay" title="Tooth decay">tooth decay</a>. Numerous studies have tried to clarify those implications, but with varying results, mainly because of the difficulty of finding populations for use as controls that consume little or no sugar. In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> recommended that adults and children reduce their intake of <a href="/wiki/Free_sugar" class="mw-redirect" title="Free sugar">free sugars</a> to less than 10%, and encouraged a reduction to below 5%, of their total <a href="/wiki/Energy_homeostasis" title="Energy homeostasis">energy intake</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_2015p4_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_2015p4-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> reflects the spread of the commodity. From <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> (<i>śarkarā</i>), meaning "ground or candied sugar," came <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> <i>shakar</i>, then to <a href="/wiki/12th_century" title="12th century">12th century</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">French</a> <i>sucre</i> and the English <i>sugar</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-oed_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oed-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The English word <i><a href="/wiki/Jaggery" title="Jaggery">jaggery</a></i>, a coarse <a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">brown sugar</a> made from <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> sap or <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> juice, has a similar etymological origin: Portuguese <i>jágara</i> from the Malayalam <i>cakkarā</i>, which is from the Sanskrit <i>śarkarā</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_sugar" title="History of sugar">History of sugar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_world_to_Renaissance">Ancient world to Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient world to Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/220px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/330px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg/440px-Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1880" data-file-height="1253" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canaviais_Sao_Paulo_01_2008_06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">Sugar cane</a> plantation</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sugar has been produced in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a><sup id="cite_ref-Moxham2002_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moxham2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> since ancient times and its cultivation spread from there into modern-day Afghanistan through the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pass" title="Khyber Pass">Khyber Pass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It was not plentiful or cheap in early times, and in most parts of the world, <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a> was more often used for sweetening. Originally, people chewed raw sugarcane to extract its sweetness. Sugarcane was a native of tropical Indian subcontinent and Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Moxham2002_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moxham2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kiple_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiple-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Different species seem to have originated from different locations with <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_barberi" title="Saccharum barberi">Saccharum barberi</a></i> originating in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_edule" title="Saccharum edule">S. edule</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_officinarum" title="Saccharum officinarum">S. officinarum</a></i> coming from <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kiple_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kiple-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sharpe_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharpe-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> One of the earliest historical references to sugarcane is in Chinese manuscripts dating to 8th century BCE, which state that the use of sugarcane originated in India.<sup id="cite_ref-gr1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gr1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the tradition of Indian medicine (<a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">āyurveda</a>), the sugarcane is known by the name <i>Ikṣu</i> and the sugarcane juice is known as <i>Phāṇita</i>. Its varieties, synonyms and characterics are defined in <a href="/wiki/Nighantu" title="Nighantu">nighaṇṭus</a> such as the Bhāvaprakāśa (1.6.23, group of sugarcanes).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
Sugar remained relatively unimportant until the Indians discovered methods of turning <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane_juice" title="Sugarcane juice">sugarcane juice</a> into granulated crystals that were easier to store and to transport.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Crystallized sugar was discovered by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Gupta_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Gupta dynasty">Imperial Guptas</a>, around the 5th century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the local Indian language, these crystals were called <i>khanda</i> (<a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>: खण्ड, <i lang="sa-Latn" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration">Khaṇḍa</i>), which is the source of the word <i>candy</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Indian sailors, who carried <a href="/wiki/Clarified_butter" title="Clarified butter">clarified butter</a> and sugar as supplies, introduced knowledge of sugar along the various <a href="/wiki/Trade_routes" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade routes">trade routes</a> they travelled.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Traveling Buddhist monks took sugar <a href="/wiki/Crystallization_methods" class="mw-redirect" title="Crystallization methods">crystallization methods</a> to China.<sup id="cite_ref-Kieschnick1_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kieschnick1-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> During the reign of <a href="/wiki/Harsha" title="Harsha">Harsha</a> (r. 606–647) in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>, Indian envoys in <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang China</a> taught methods of cultivating sugarcane after <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a> (r. 626–649) made known his interest in sugar. China established its first sugarcane plantations in the seventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-sen_38_40_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sen_38_40-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Chinese documents confirm at least two missions to India, initiated in 647 CE, to obtain technology for sugar refining.<sup id="cite_ref-Kieschnick11_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kieschnick11-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the Indian subcontinent,<sup id="cite_ref-Moxham2002_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moxham2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> the Middle East and China, sugar became a staple of cooking and desserts.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg/220px-Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg/330px-Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg/440px-Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="662" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Trionfi_di_Cibele_e_Juno.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two elaborate sugar <i><a href="/wiki/Trionfo" title="Trionfo">triomfi</a></i> of goddesses for a dinner given by the <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Castlemaine" title="Earl of Castlemaine">Earl of Castlemaine</a>, British Ambassador in Rome, 1687</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, admiral of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Macedonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander of Macedonia">Alexander of Macedonia</a>, knew of sugar during the year 325 B.C., because of his participation in <a href="/wiki/Indian_campaign_of_Alexander_the_Great#Sources" title="Indian campaign of Alexander the Great">the campaign of India</a> led by Alexander (<i><a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anabasis_Alexandri" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabasis Alexandri">Anabasis</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Pedanius_Dioscorides" title="Pedanius Dioscorides">Pedanius Dioscorides</a> in the 1st century CE described sugar in his medical treatise <a href="/wiki/De_Materia_Medica" title="De Materia Medica">De Materia Medica</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, a 1st-century CE Roman, described sugar in his <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Natural History</a>: "Sugar is made in Arabia as well, but Indian sugar is better. It is a kind of honey found in cane, white as gum, and it crunches between the teeth. It comes in lumps the size of a hazelnut. Sugar is used only for medical purposes."<sup id="cite_ref-faas_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faas-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusaders</a> brought sugar back to Europe after their campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, where they encountered caravans carrying "sweet salt". Early in the 12th century, Venice acquired some villages near <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a> and set up estates to produce sugar for export to Europe. It supplemented the use of honey, which had previously been the only available sweetener.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponting_2000_481_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponting_2000_481-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Crusade chronicler <a href="/wiki/William_of_Tyre" title="William of Tyre">William of Tyre</a>, writing in the late 12th century, described sugar as "very necessary for the use and health of mankind".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> In the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> was the chief sugar refining and distribution center in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-gr1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gr1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There was a drastic change in the mid-15th century, when <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> were settled from Europe, and sugar grown there.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>   After this an "all-consuming passion for sugar ... swept through society" as it became far more easily available, though initially still very expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  By 1492, Madeira was producing over three million pounds weight of sugar annually.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>  <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, one of the centers of distribution, became known for candied fruit, while Venice specialized in pastries, sweets (candies), and <a href="/wiki/Sugar_sculpture" title="Sugar sculpture">sugar sculptures</a>.  Sugar was considered to have "valuable medicinal properties" as a "warm" food under prevailing categories, being "helpful to the stomach, to cure cold diseases, and sooth lung complaints".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A feast given in <a href="/wiki/Tours" title="Tours">Tours</a> in 1457 by <a href="/wiki/Gaston_IV,_Count_of_Foix" title="Gaston IV, Count of Foix">Gaston de Foix</a>, which is "probably the best and most complete account we have of a late medieval banquet" includes the first mention of sugar sculptures, as the final food brought in was "a heraldic menagerie sculpted in sugar: lions, stags, monkeys ... each holding in paw or beak the arms of the Hungarian king".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>  Other recorded grand feasts in the decades following included similar pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  Originally the sculptures seem to have been eaten in the meal, but later they become merely table decorations, the most elaborate called <i><a href="/wiki/Trionfo" title="Trionfo">triomfi</a></i>. Several significant sculptors are known to have produced them; in some cases their preliminary drawings survive. Early ones were in brown sugar, partly <a href="/wiki/Casting" title="Casting">cast</a> in molds, with the final touches carved.  They continued to be used until at least the Coronation Banquet for <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VII of the United Kingdom">Edward VII of the United Kingdom</a> in 1903; among other sculptures every guest was given a sugar crown to take away.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/220px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/330px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg/440px-George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="824" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Flegel_Still-Life_with_Bread_and_Confectionary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Still-Life" class="mw-redirect" title="Still-Life">Still-Life</a> with Bread and Confectionery</i>, by <a href="/wiki/George_Flegel" class="mw-redirect" title="George Flegel">George Flegel</a>, first half of 17th century</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">Triangular trade</a></div>
<p>In August 1492 <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> stopped at <a href="/wiki/La_Gomera" title="La Gomera">La Gomera</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> for wine and water, intending to stay only four days. He became romantically involved with the governor of the island, <a href="/wiki/Beatriz_de_Bobadilla_y_Ossorio" title="Beatriz de Bobadilla y Ossorio">Beatriz de Bobadilla y Ossorio</a>, and stayed a month. When he finally sailed, she gave him cuttings of sugarcane, which he carried to the New World. This was the introduction of this plant.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The cuttings were planted and the first sugar-cane harvest in <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> took place in 1501. Many sugar mills had been constructed in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> by the 1520s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenitez-Rojo199693_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenitez-Rojo199693-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The Portuguese took sugar cane to <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. By 1540, there were 800 cane-sugar mills in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_Island" title="Santa Catarina Island">Santa Catarina Island</a> and another 2,000 on the north coast of Brazil, <a href="/wiki/Demarara" class="mw-redirect" title="Demarara">Demarara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surinam" class="mw-redirect" title="Surinam">Surinam</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/220px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/330px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg/440px-Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="918" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hacienda_La_Fortuna_Francisco_Oller_1885_Brooklyn_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Hacienda La Fortuna.</i> A sugar mill complex in Puerto Rico, painted by <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Oller" title="Francisco Oller">Francisco Oller</a> in 1885. <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Sugar was a luxury in Europe until the 18th century, when it became more widely available. It became highly popular and by the 19th century, sugar came to be considered<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (September 2018)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> a necessity. This evolution of taste and demand for sugar as an essential food ingredient resulted in major economic and social changes.<sup id="cite_ref-mintz_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mintz-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Demand drove, in part, the colonization of tropical islands and areas where labor-intensive sugarcane plantations and sugar manufacturing could be successful. The demand for cheap labor to perform the labor-intensive cultivation and processing increased the demand for the slave trade from Africa (in particular West Africa).
</p><p>After slavery was abolished, the demand for workers in the British Caribbean colonies was filled by <a href="/wiki/Indentured_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured labor">indentured laborers</a> from Indian subcontinent (in particular India).<sup id="cite_ref-britain1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britain1-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Millions of slave and indentured laborers were brought into the Caribbean and the Americas, Indian Ocean colonies, southeast Asia, Pacific Islands, and East Africa and Natal. Thus the modern ethnic mix of many nations that have been settled in the last two centuries has been influenced by the demand for sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg/130px-Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg" decoding="async" width="130" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg/195px-Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg/260px-Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_Cane_closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This is a close-up image of sugar cane. Demand for sugar helped create the <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial system</a> in areas where cultivation of sugar cane was profitable.</div></div></div>
<p>Sugar also led to some industrialization of areas where sugar cane was grown. For example, in the 1790s Lieutenant J. Paterson, of the Bengal establishment, promoted to the British Government the idea that sugar cane could grow in British India, where it had started, with many advantages and at less expense than in the West Indies. As a result, sugar factories were established in <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> in eastern India.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
During the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, sugar-beet production increased in continental Europe because of the difficulty of importing sugar when shipping was subject to <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a>. By 1880 the sugar beet was the main source of sugar in Europe. It was also cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a> and other parts of England, although the United Kingdom continued to import the main part of its sugar from its colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the late nineteenth century, sugar was purchased in <a href="/wiki/Sugarloaf" title="Sugarloaf">loaves</a>, which had to be cut using implements called <a href="/wiki/Sugar_nips" title="Sugar nips">sugar nips</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In later years, granulated sugar was more usually sold in bags. Sugar cubes were produced in the nineteenth century. The first inventor of a process to produce sugar in cube form was <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravian</a> <a href="/wiki/Jakub_Kry%C5%A1tof_Rad" class="mw-redirect" title="Jakub Kryštof Rad">Jakub Kryštof Rad</a>, director of a sugar company in <a href="/wiki/Da%C4%8Dice" title="Dačice">Dačice</a>. He began sugar-cube production after being granted a five-year patent for the process on January 23, 1843. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Tate" title="Henry Tate">Henry Tate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tate_%26_Lyle" title="Tate &amp; Lyle">Tate &amp; Lyle</a> was another early manufacturer of sugar cubes at his refineries in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. Tate purchased a patent for sugar-cube manufacture from German <a href="/wiki/Eugen_Langen" title="Eugen Langen">Eugen Langen</a>, who in 1872 had invented a different method of processing of sugar cubes.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sugar was rationed during World War I and more sharply during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  This led to the development and use of various <a href="/wiki/Artificial_sweeteners" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial sweeteners">artificial sweeteners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Saccharose.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Saccharose.svg/320px-Saccharose.svg.png" decoding="async" width="320" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">Sucrose</a>: a disaccharide of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a> (right), important molecules in the body.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">Carbohydrate</a></div>
<p>Scientifically, <i>sugar</i> loosely refers to a number of carbohydrates, such as monosaccharides, disaccharides, or <a href="/wiki/Oligosaccharide" title="Oligosaccharide">oligosaccharides</a>. Monosaccharides are also called "simple sugars," the most important being glucose. Most monosaccharides have a formula that conforms to <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2n</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">n</sub></span></span> with n between 3 and 7 (<a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> being an exception). <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a> has the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>. The names of typical sugars end with -<i>ose</i>, as in "<a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>". Sometimes such words may also refer to any types of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a> soluble in water. The <a href="/wiki/Open-chain_compound" title="Open-chain compound">acyclic</a> mono- and disaccharides contain either <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehyde</a> groups or <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketone</a> groups. These <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonyl">carbon-oxygen</a> double bonds (C=O) are the reactive centers. All saccharides with more than one ring in their structure result from two or more monosaccharides joined by <a href="/wiki/Glycosidic_bond" title="Glycosidic bond">glycosidic bonds</a> with the resultant loss of a molecule of water (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) per bond.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Monosaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Monosaccharides">Monosaccharides</a> in a closed-chain form can form glycosidic bonds with other monosaccharides, creating disaccharides (such as sucrose) and polysaccharides (such as <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a>). <a href="/wiki/Enzymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzymes">Enzymes</a> must hydrolyze or otherwise break these glycosidic bonds before such compounds become <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolized</a>. After digestion and absorption the principal monosaccharides present in the blood and internal tissues include glucose, fructose, and galactose. Many <a href="/wiki/Pentose" title="Pentose">pentoses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hexose" title="Hexose">hexoses</a> can form <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_compound" title="Heterocyclic compound">ring structures</a>. In these closed-chain forms, the aldehyde or ketone group remains non-free, so many of the reactions typical of these groups cannot occur. Glucose in solution exists mostly in the ring form at <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equilibrium" title="Chemical equilibrium">equilibrium</a>, with less than 0.1% of the molecules in the open-chain form.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_polymers">Natural polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Natural polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">Biopolymers</a> of sugars are common in nature. Through photosynthesis, plants produce <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate">glyceraldehyde-3-phosphate</a> (G3P), a phosphated 3-carbon sugar that is used by the cell to make monosaccharides such as glucose (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>) or (as in cane and beet) sucrose (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">12</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">22</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">11</sub></span></span>). Monosaccharides may be further converted into <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides#structural_polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">structural polysaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> construction or into energy reserves in the form of <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharides#storage_polysaccharides" class="mw-redirect" title="Polysaccharides">storage polysaccharides</a> such as starch or <a href="/wiki/Inulin" title="Inulin">inulin</a>. Starch, consisting of two different polymers of glucose, is a readily degradable form of chemical <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">energy</a> stored by <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, and can be converted to other types of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-Pigman_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigman-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Another polymer of glucose is cellulose, which is a linear chain composed of several hundred or thousand glucose units. It is used by plants as a structural component in their cell walls. Humans can digest cellulose only to a very limited extent, though <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a> can do so with the help of <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> bacteria in their gut.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> are built up of the monosaccharides <a href="/wiki/Deoxyribose" title="Deoxyribose">deoxyribose</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Ribose" title="Ribose">ribose</a>, respectively. Deoxyribose has the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and ribose the formula <span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability_and_heat_response">Flammability and heat response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flammability and heat response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/220px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/330px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Sugar_2xmacro.jpg/440px-Sugar_2xmacro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sugar_2xmacro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Magnification of grains of refined <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, the most common free sugar.</div></div></div>
<p>Because sugars burn easily when exposed to flame, the handling of sugars risks <a href="/wiki/Dust_explosion" title="Dust explosion">dust explosion</a>. The risk of explosion is higher when the sugar has been milled to superfine texture, such as for use in <a href="/wiki/Chewing_gum" title="Chewing gum">chewing gum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2008_Georgia_sugar_refinery_explosion" title="2008 Georgia sugar refinery explosion">2008 Georgia sugar refinery explosion</a>, which killed 14 people and injured 40, and destroyed most of the refinery, was caused by the ignition of sugar dust.
</p><p>In its culinary use, exposing sugar to heat causes <a href="/wiki/Caramelization" title="Caramelization">caramelization</a>. As the process occurs, <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Diacetyl" title="Diacetyl">diacetyl</a> are released, producing the characteristic <a href="/wiki/Caramel" title="Caramel">caramel</a> flavor.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monosaccharides">Monosaccharides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Monosaccharides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fructose, galactose, and glucose are all simple sugars, <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a>, with the general formula C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>12</sub>O<sub>6</sub>. They have five hydroxyl groups (−OH) and a carbonyl group (C=O) and are cyclic when dissolved in water. They each exist as several <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomers</a> with dextro- and laevo-rotatory forms that cause polarized light to diverge to the right or the left.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">Fructose</a></b>, or fruit sugar, occurs naturally in fruits, some root vegetables, cane sugar and honey and is the sweetest of the sugars. It is one of the components of sucrose or table sugar. It is used as a <a href="/wiki/High-fructose_corn_syrup" title="High-fructose corn syrup">high-fructose syrup</a>, which is manufactured from hydrolyzed corn starch that has been processed to yield <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a>, with enzymes then added to convert part of the glucose into fructose.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Galactose" title="Galactose">Galactose</a></b> generally does not occur in the free state but is a constituent with glucose of the disaccharide <a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">lactose</a> or milk sugar. It is less sweet than glucose. It is a component of the antigens found on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a> that determine <a href="/wiki/ABO_blood_group_system" title="ABO blood group system">blood groups</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Raven_and_Johnson_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raven_and_Johnson-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">Glucose</a></b>, dextrose or grape sugar, occurs naturally in fruits and plant juices and is the primary product of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Most ingested carbohydrates are converted into glucose during digestion and it is the form of sugar that is transported around the bodies of animals in the bloodstream. Glucose syrup is a liquid form of glucose that is widely used in the manufacture of foodstuffs. It can be manufactured from starch by <a href="/wiki/Enzymatic_hydrolysis" title="Enzymatic hydrolysis">enzymatic hydrolysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disaccharides">Disaccharides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Disaccharides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lactose, maltose, and sucrose are all compound sugars, <a href="/wiki/Disaccharide" title="Disaccharide">disaccharides</a>, with the general formula C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>22</sub>O<sub>11</sub>. They are formed by the combination of two monosaccharide molecules with the exclusion of a molecule of water.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Lactose" title="Lactose">Lactose</a></b> is the naturally occurring sugar found in milk. A molecule of lactose is formed by the combination of a molecule of galactose with a molecule of glucose. It is broken down when consumed into its constituent parts by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Lactase" title="Lactase">lactase</a> during digestion. Children have this enzyme but some adults no longer form it and they are unable to digest lactose.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Maltose" title="Maltose">Maltose</a></b> is formed during the germination of certain grains, the most notable being <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, which is converted into <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a>, the source of the sugar's name. A molecule of maltose is formed by the combination of two molecules of glucose. It is less sweet than glucose, fructose or sucrose.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It is formed in the body during the digestion of starch by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a> and is itself broken down during digestion by the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Maltase" title="Maltase">maltase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">Sucrose</a></b> is found in the stems of sugarcane and roots of sugar beet. It also occurs naturally alongside fructose and glucose in other plants, in particular fruits and some roots such as carrots. The different proportions of sugars found in these foods determines the range of sweetness experienced when eating them.<sup id="cite_ref-Manual_52-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manual-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> A molecule of sucrose is formed by the combination of a molecule of glucose with a molecule of fructose. After being eaten, sucrose is split into its constituent parts during digestion by a number of enzymes known as <a href="/wiki/Sucrase" title="Sucrase">sucrases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The sugar contents of common fruits and vegetables are presented in Table 1.
The fructose to fructose plus glucose ratio is calculated by including the fructose and glucose coming from the sucrose.
</p>

<p>Due to rising demand, sugar production in general increased some 14% over the period 2009 to 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-usda17_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda17-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The largest importers were China, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-usda17_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usda17-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugar_beet">Sugar beet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sugar beet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2016, global production of <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beets</a> was 277 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, led by Russia with 19% of the world total (table).
</p><p>The sugar beet became a major source of sugar in the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a> when methods for extracting the sugar became available. It is a <a href="/wiki/Biennial_plant" title="Biennial plant">biennial plant</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivated variety</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Beta_vulgaris" title="Beta vulgaris">Beta vulgaris</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Amaranthaceae" title="Amaranthaceae">Amaranthaceae</a>, the tuberous root of which contains a high proportion of sucrose. It is cultivated as a root crop in temperate regions with adequate rainfall and requires a fertile soil. The crop is harvested mechanically in the autumn and the crown of leaves and excess soil removed. The roots do not deteriorate rapidly and may be left in the field for some weeks before being transported to the processing plant where the crop is washed and sliced, and the sugar extracted by diffusion.<sup id="cite_ref-skil_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Milk_of_lime" class="mw-redirect" title="Milk of lime">Milk of lime</a> is added to the raw juice with <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">calcium carbonate</a>. After water is evaporated by boiling the syrup under a vacuum, the syrup is cooled and seeded with sugar crystals. The <a href="/wiki/White_sugar" title="White sugar">white sugar</a> that crystallizes can be separated in a centrifuge and dried, requiring no further refining.<sup id="cite_ref-skil_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sugarcane">Sugarcane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sugarcane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; clear:left; width:18em;">
<tbody><tr>

<th style="background:#ddf; width:75%;">Country

<td><center><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/23px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/35px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Thailand.svg/45px-Flag_of_Thailand.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a></center></td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2"><center><small>Source: <a href="/wiki/FAOSTAT" class="mw-redirect" title="FAOSTAT">FAOSTAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a><sup id="cite_ref-faostat-sc16_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat-sc16-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></small></center>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Global production of <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> in 2016 was 1.9 billion tonnes, with Brazil producing 41% of the world total and India 18% (table).
</p><p>Sugarcane refers to any of several species, or their hybrids, of giant grasses in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum" title="Saccharum">Saccharum</a></i> in the family <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Poaceae</a>. They have been cultivated in tropical climates in the Indian subcontinent and Southeast Asia over centuries for the sucrose found in their stems.<sup id="cite_ref-Moxham2002_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moxham2002-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> A great expansion in sugarcane production took place in the <a href="/wiki/18th_century" title="18th century">18th century</a> with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Slave_plantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave plantation">slave plantations</a> in the Americas. The use of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> for the labor-intensive process resulted in sugar production, enabling prices cheap enough for most people to buy. Mechanization reduced some labor needs, but in the <a href="/wiki/21st_century" title="21st century">21st century</a>, cultivation and production relied on low-wage laborers.
</p><p>Sugar cane requires a frost-free climate with sufficient rainfall during the growing season to make full use of the plant's substantial growth potential. The crop is harvested mechanically or by hand, chopped into lengths and conveyed rapidly to the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar mill">processing plant</a> (commonly known as a <a href="/wiki/Sugar_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar mill">sugar mill</a>) where it is either milled and the juice extracted with water or extracted by diffusion.<sup id="cite_ref-skil2_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The juice is clarified with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">lime</a> and heated to destroy <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. The resulting thin syrup is concentrated in a series of evaporators, after which further water is removed. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturated</a> solution is seeded with sugar crystals, facilitating crystal formation and drying.<sup id="cite_ref-skil2_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">Molasses</a> is a by-product of the process and the fiber from the stems, known as <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-skil2_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> is burned to provide energy for the sugar extraction process. The crystals of raw sugar have a sticky brown coating and either can be used as they are, can be bleached by <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, or can be treated in a <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">carbonatation</a> process to produce a whiter product.<sup id="cite_ref-skil2_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skil2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> About 2,500 litres (660&#160;US&#160;gal) of irrigation water is needed for every one kilogram (2.2 pounds) of sugar produced.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refining">Refining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Refining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sugar_refinery" title="Sugar refinery">Sugar refinery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-centrifugal_cane_sugar" title="Non-centrifugal cane sugar">Non-centrifugal cane sugar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/White_sugar" title="White sugar">White sugar</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_sugar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Russian_sugar.jpg/220px-Russian_sugar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Russian_sugar.jpg/330px-Russian_sugar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Russian_sugar.jpg/440px-Russian_sugar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1165" data-file-height="1165" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russian_sugar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pack of sugar made from sugar beet</div></div></div>
<p>Refined sugar is made from raw sugar that has undergone a <a href="/wiki/Refining" title="Refining">refining</a> process to remove the <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-agrifinasi_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agrifinasi-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kompas_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kompas-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Raw sugar is <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> which is extracted from sugarcane or <a href="/wiki/Sugar_beet" title="Sugar beet">sugar beet</a>. While raw sugar can be consumed, the refining process removes unwanted tastes and results in refined sugar or <a href="/wiki/White_sugar" title="White sugar">white sugar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-western_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-western-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tebu_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sugar may be transported in bulk to the country where it will be used and the refining process often takes place there. The first stage is known as affination and involves immersing the sugar crystals in a concentrated syrup that softens and removes the sticky brown coating without dissolving them. The crystals are then separated from the liquor and dissolved in water. The resulting syrup is treated either by a <a href="/wiki/Carbonatation" title="Carbonatation">carbonatation</a> or by a phosphatation process. Both involve the precipitation of a fine solid in the syrup and when this is filtered out, many of the impurities are removed at the same time. Removal of color is achieved by using either a granular <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Ion-exchange_resin" title="Ion-exchange resin">ion-exchange resin</a>. The sugar syrup is concentrated by boiling and then cooled and seeded with sugar crystals, causing the sugar to crystallize out. The liquor is spun off in a centrifuge and the white crystals are dried in hot air and ready to be packaged or used. The surplus liquor is made into refiners' molasses.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_for_Uniform_Methods_of_Sugar_Analysis" title="International Commission for Uniform Methods of Sugar Analysis">International Commission for Uniform Methods of Sugar Analysis</a> sets standards for the measurement of the purity of refined sugar, known as ICUMSA numbers; lower numbers indicate a higher level of purity in the refined sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Refined sugar is widely used for industrial needs for higher quality. Refined sugar is purer (ICUMSA below 300) than raw sugar (ICUMSA over 1,500).<sup id="cite_ref-tebu_2_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu_2-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The level of purity associated with the colors of sugar, expressed by standard number <a href="/wiki/International_Commission_for_Uniform_Methods_of_Sugar_Analysis" title="International Commission for Uniform Methods of Sugar Analysis">ICUMSA</a>, the smaller ICUMSA numbers indicate the higher purity of sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-tebu_2_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tebu_2-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_and_uses">Forms and uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Forms and uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/170px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/255px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg/340px-Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1790" data-file-height="2760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rock-Candy-Closeup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rock_candy" title="Rock candy">Rock candy</a> crystallized out of a <a href="/wiki/Supersaturation" title="Supersaturation">supersaturated sugar solution</a> that contains green dye.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brown_sugar" title="Brown sugar">Brown sugars</a> are granulated sugars, either containing residual molasses, or with the grains deliberately coated with molasses to produce a light- or dark-colored sugar. They are used in baked goods, confectionery, and <a href="/wiki/Toffee" title="Toffee">toffees</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Granulated sugars are used at the table, to sprinkle on foods and to sweeten hot drinks (coffee and tea), and in home baking to add sweetness and texture to baked products (cookies and cakes) and desserts (pudding and ice cream). They are also used as a preservative to prevent micro-organisms from growing and perishable food from spoiling, as in candied fruits, jams, and <a href="/wiki/Marmalade" title="Marmalade">marmalades</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inverted_sugar_syrup" title="Inverted sugar syrup">Invert sugars</a> and syrups are blended to manufacturers specifications and are used in breads, cakes, and beverages for adjusting sweetness, aiding moisture retention and avoiding crystallization of sugars.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Liquid sugars are strong syrups consisting of 67% granulated sugar dissolved in water. They are used in the food processing of a wide range of products including beverages, <a href="/wiki/Hard_candy" title="Hard candy">hard candy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream" title="Ice cream">ice cream</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">jams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Low-calorie sugars and artificial sweeteners are often made of <a href="/wiki/Maltodextrin" title="Maltodextrin">maltodextrin</a> with added sweeteners. Maltodextrin is an easily digestible synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> consisting of short chains of glucose molecules and is made by the partial <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> of starch.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Milled sugars (known as confectioner's sugar and powdered sugar) are ground to a fine powder. They are used as <a href="/wiki/Powdered_sugar" title="Powdered sugar">powdered sugar</a> (also known as icing sugar or confectionary sugar), for dusting foods and in baking and confectionery.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">Molasses</a> is commonly used to make <a href="/wiki/Rum" title="Rum">rum</a>, and sugar byproducts are used to make <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> for fuel.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Polyol" title="Polyol">Polyols</a> are <a href="/wiki/Sugar_alcohol" title="Sugar alcohol">sugar alcohols</a> and are used in chewing gums where a sweet flavor is required that lasts for a prolonged time in the mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-Union1990_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Union1990-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Screened sugars are crystalline products separated according to the size of the grains. They are used for decorative table sugars, for blending in dry mixes and in baking and confectionery.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Sugar cubes<sup>(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/W%C3%BCrfelzucker" class="extiw" title="de:Würfelzucker">de</a>)</sup> (sometimes called sugar lumps) are white or brown granulated sugars lightly steamed and pressed together in block shape. They are used to sweeten drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg/220px-W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg/330px-W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg/440px-W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5349" data-file-height="3343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:W%C3%BCrfelzucker_--_2018_--_3564.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sugar cubes</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sugarloaf" title="Sugarloaf">Sugarloaf</a> was the usual <a href="/wiki/Cone" title="Cone">cone</a>-form in which refined sugar was produced and sold until the late 19th century. This shape is still in use in Germany (for preparation of <i><a href="/wiki/Feuerzangenbowle" title="Feuerzangenbowle">Feuerzangenbowle</a></i>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syrup" title="Syrup">Syrups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treacle" title="Treacle">treacles</a> are dissolved invert sugars heated to develop the characteristic flavors. (Treacles have added molasses.) They are used in a range of baked goods and confectionery including toffees and <a href="/wiki/Licorice" class="mw-redirect" title="Licorice">licorice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BS_74-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BS-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugars_in_wine" title="Sugars in wine">fruit sugars</a> are converted into alcohol by a <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermentation</a> process. If the <a href="/wiki/Must" title="Must">must</a> formed by pressing the fruit has a low sugar content, additional sugar may be added to raise the alcohol content of the wine in a process called <a href="/wiki/Chaptalization" title="Chaptalization">chaptalization</a>. In the production of sweet wines, fermentation may be halted before it has run its full course, leaving behind some <a href="/wiki/Sweetness_of_wine" title="Sweetness of wine">residual sugar</a> that gives the wine its sweet taste.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_pg_665-666_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_pg_665-666-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In most parts of the world, sugar is an important part of the human diet, making food more palatable and providing <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a>. After cereals and vegetable oils, sugar derived from sugarcane and beet provided more kilocalories per capita per day on average than other food groups.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> According to one source, <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> consumption of sugar in 2016 was highest in the United States, followed by Germany and the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition_and_flavor">Nutrition and flavor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sugar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nutrition and flavor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
